<p>The <b>golden eagle</b> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) is one of the best-known <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. It is the most widely distributed species of <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>. Like all eagles, it belongs to the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>. These birds are dark brown, with lighter golden-brown plumage on their <a href="/wiki/Nape" title="Nape">napes</a>. Immature eagles of this species typically have white on the tail and often have white markings on the wings. Golden eagles use their agility and speed combined with powerful feet and massive, sharp talons to snatch up a variety of prey, mainly <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marmot" title="Marmot">marmots</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
Golden eagles maintain home ranges or territories that may be as large as 200&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (77&#160;sq&#160;mi). They build large <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nests</a> in cliffs and other high places to which they may return for several breeding years. Most breeding activities take place in the spring; they are monogamous and may remain together for several years or possibly for life. Females lay up to four <a href="/wiki/Bird_egg" title="Bird egg">eggs</a>, and then incubate them for six weeks. Typically, one or two young survive to fledge in about three months. These juvenile golden eagles usually attain full independence in the fall, after which they wander widely until establishing a territory for themselves in four to five years.
</p><p>Once widespread across the <a href="/wiki/Holarctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Holarctic">Holarctic</a>, it has disappeared from many areas which are now more heavily populated by humans. Despite being <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpated</a> from or uncommon in some of its former range, the species is still widespread, being present in sizeable stretches of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>. It is the largest and least populous of the five species of true accipitrid to occur as a breeding species in both the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic_realm" title="Palearctic realm">Palearctic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nearctic_realm" title="Nearctic realm">Nearctic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For centuries, this species has been one of the most highly regarded birds used in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>. Due to its hunting prowess, the golden eagle is regarded with great mystic reverence in some ancient, tribal cultures.  It is one of the most extensively studied species of raptor in the world in some parts of its range, such as the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Palearctic">Western Palearctic</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png/220px-GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png/330px-GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png/440px-GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="583" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldenEagleFootLyd4.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The foot and talons of a golden eagle</div></div></div>
<p>The golden eagle is a very large raptor, 66 to 102 centimetres (26 to 40&#160;in) in length.  Its wings are broad and the wingspan is 1.8 to 2.34 metres (5&#160;ft 11&#160;in to 7&#160;ft 8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Cornell_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles' wingspan is the fifth largest among living eagle species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">Females are larger than males</a>, with a bigger difference in larger subspecies. Females of the large Himalayan golden eagles are about 37% heavier than males and have nearly 9% longer wings, whereas in the smaller Japanese golden eagles, females are only 26% heavier with around 6% longer wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Terres478_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres478-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the largest subspecies (<i>A. c. daphanea</i>), males and females weigh typically 4.05 kilograms (8.9&#160;lb) and 6.35&#160;kg (14.0&#160;lb), respectively. In the smallest subspecies, <i>A. c. japonica</i>, males weigh 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb) and females 3.25&#160;kg (7.2&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the species overall, males average around 3.6&#160;kg (7.9&#160;lb) and females around 5.1&#160;kg (11&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The maximum size of golden eagles is debated. Large subspecies are the heaviest representatives of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> and this species is on average the seventh-heaviest living eagle species. The golden eagle is the second heaviest breeding eagle in North America, Europe and Africa and the fourth heaviest in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> For some time, the largest known mass authenticated for a wild female was the specimen from the <i>A. c. chrysaetos</i> subspecies which weighed around 6.7&#160;kg (15&#160;lb) and spanned 2.55&#160;m (8&#160;ft 4&#160;in) across the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> American golden eagles are typically somewhat smaller than the large Eurasian species, but a massive female that was banded and released in 2006 around Wyoming's <a href="/wiki/Bridger-Teton_National_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridger-Teton National Forest">Bridger-Teton National Forest</a> became the heaviest wild golden eagle on record, at 7.7&#160;kg (17&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Golden_Eagle_May_Set_Record_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Golden_Eagle_May_Set_Record-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  Captive birds have been measured with a wingspan of 2.81&#160;m (9&#160;ft 3&#160;in) and a mass of 12.1&#160;kg (27&#160;lb), though this mass was for an eagle bred for <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>, which tend to be unnaturally heavy.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard measurements of the species include a <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> length of 52–72&#160;cm (20–28&#160;in), a tail length of 26.5–38&#160;cm (10.4–15.0&#160;in) and a <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> length of 9.4–12.2&#160;cm (3.7–4.8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Culmen_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Culmen (bird)">culmen</a> (upper ridge of beak) reportedly averages around 4.5&#160;cm (1.8&#160;in), with a range of 3.6 to 5&#160;cm (1.4 to 2.0&#160;in). The bill length from the <a href="/wiki/Gape" class="mw-redirect" title="Gape">gape</a> measures around 6&#160;cm (2.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The long, straight and powerful <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">hallux-claw</a> (hind claw) can range from 4.5 to 6.34&#160;cm (1.77 to 2.50&#160;in), about one centimetre longer than in a <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> and a little more than one centimetre less than a <a href="/wiki/Harpy_eagle" title="Harpy eagle">harpy eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bortolotti_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bortolotti-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colour">Colour</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg/220px-Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg/330px-Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg/440px-Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="733" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Orel_skaln%C3%AD_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subadult, showing white in tail and dark neck</div></div></div>
<p>Adults of both sexes have similar plumage and are primarily dark brown, with some grey on the inner wing and tail, and a paler, typically golden colour on the back of the crown and nape that gives the species its common name.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  Unlike other <i>Aquila</i> species, where the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsal</a> feathers are typically similar in colour to the rest of the plumage, the tarsal feathers of golden eagles tend to be paler, ranging from light golden to white.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In addition, some full-grown birds (especially in North America) have white "<a href="/wiki/Epaulettes" class="mw-redirect" title="Epaulettes">epaulettes</a>" on the upper part of each scapular <a href="/wiki/Feather_tract" class="mw-redirect" title="Feather tract">feather tract</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jollie_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jollie-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The bill is dark at the tip, fading to a lighter horn colour, with a yellow <a href="/wiki/Cere" class="mw-redirect" title="Cere">cere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Like many <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">accipitrids</a>, the bare portion of the feet is yellow.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> There are subtle differences in colouration among subspecies, described <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle#Subspecies_and_distribution" title="Golden eagle">below</a>.
</p><p>Juvenile golden eagles are similar to adults but tend to be darker, appearing black on the back especially in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. They have a less faded colour. Young birds are white for about two-thirds of their tail length, ending with a broad, black band.<sup id="cite_ref-Terres478_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Terres478-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, juvenile eagles have white patches on the <a href="/wiki/Remiges" class="mw-redirect" title="Remiges">remiges</a> at the bases of the inner primaries and the outer secondaries, forming a crescent marking on the wings which tends to be divided by darker feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABAaging_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABAaging-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Rarely, juvenile birds may have only traces of white on the tail. Compared to the relatively consistently white tail, the white patches on the wing are extremely variable; some juveniles have almost no white visible. Juveniles of less than 12 months of age tend to have the most white in their plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-Jollie_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jollie-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> By their second summer, the white underwing coverts are usually replaced by a characteristic rusty brown colour. By the third summer, the upper-wing coverts are largely replaced by dark brown feathers, although not all feathers moult at once which leaves many juvenile birds with a grizzled pattern. The tail follows a similar pattern of maturation to the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Jollie_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jollie-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABAaging_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABAaging-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Due to the variability between individuals, juvenile eagles cannot be reliably aged by sight alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Juvenile_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Juvenile-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Many golden eagles still have white on the tail during their first attempt at nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The final adult plumage is not fully attained until the birds are between 5 and a half and 6 and a half years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Juvenile_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Juvenile-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moulting">Moulting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg/220px-GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg/330px-GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg/440px-GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2084" data-file-height="1900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GoldenEagle-Nova.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captive adult of the North American subspecies <i>Aquila chrysaetos canadensis</i></div></div></div>
<p>This species moults gradually beginning in March or April until September or October each year. Moulting usually decreases in winter. Moult of the contour feathers begins on the head and neck region and progresses along the feather tracts in a general front-to-back direction. Feathers on head, neck, back and scapulars may be replaced annually. With large feathers of the wing and tail, moult begins with the innermost feathers and proceeds outwards in a straightforward manner known as "descendant" moult.<sup id="cite_ref-Jollie_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jollie-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalisations">Vocalisations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="25.143922902494" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Golden_Eagle_(Aquila_chrysaetos)_(W1CDR0001387_BD6).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/cf/Golden_Eagle_%28Aquila_chrysaetos%29_%28W1CDR0001387_BD6%29.ogg/Golden_Eagle_%28Aquila_chrysaetos%29_%28W1CDR0001387_BD6%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="127832" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Golden_Eagle_%28Aquila_chrysaetos%29_%28W1CDR0001387_BD6%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (197 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="197147" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_(Aquila_chrysaetos)_(W1CDR0001387_BD6).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calls of a golden eagle, recorded at Kinlochewe, Scotland, July 1969</div></div></div>
<p>While many accipitrids are not known for their strong voices, golden eagles have a particular tendency for silence, even while breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> That being said, some vocalization has been recorded, usually centering around the nesting period. The voice of the golden eagle is considered weak, high, and shrill, has been called "quite pathetic" and "puppy-like", and seems incongruous with the formidable size and nature of the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Most known vocalisations seem to function as contact calls between eagles, sometimes adults to their offspring, occasionally territorial birds to intruders and rarely between a breeding pair. In western <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, nine distinct calls were noted: a <i>chirp</i>, a <i>seeir</i>, a <i>pssa</i>, a <i>skonk</i>, a <i>cluck</i>, a <i>wonk</i>, a <i>honk</i> and a <i>hiss</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">Flight</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg/220px-Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg/330px-Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg/440px-Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3452" data-file-height="3224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_in_flight_-_5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In flight</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg/220px-Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg/330px-Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg/440px-Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2806" data-file-height="1879" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_flying.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden eagle flying in dihedral with food</div></div></div>
<p>Golden eagles are sometimes considered the best fliers among eagles and perhaps among all raptorial birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> They are equipped with broad, long wings with somewhat finger-like indentations on the tips of the wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles are unique among their genus in that they often fly in a slight <a href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aeronautics)" title="Dihedral (aeronautics)">dihedral</a>, which means the wings are often held in a slight, upturned V-shape.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> When they need to flap, golden eagles appear at their most laboured, but this is less common than soaring or gliding––.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Flapping flight usually consists of 6–8 deep wing-beats, interspersed with 2–3 second glides.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> While soaring, the wings and tail are held in one plane with the primary tips often spread.<sup id="cite_ref-SanDiego_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SanDiego-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> A typical, unhurried soaring speed in golden eagles is around 45–52 kilometres per hour (28–32&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-ADW_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADW-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> When hunting or displaying, the golden eagle can glide very fast, reaching speeds of up to 190 kilometres per hour (120&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SanDiego_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SanDiego-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> When stooping (diving) in the direction of prey or during territorial displays, the eagle holds its legs up against its tail, and holds its wings tight and partially closed against its body. When diving after prey, a golden eagle can reach 240 to 320 kilometres per hour (150 to 200&#160;mph). Although less agile and manoeuvrable, the golden eagle is apparently quite the equal and possibly even the superior of the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>'s stooping and gliding speeds.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This makes the golden eagle one of the two fastest living animals.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although most flight in golden eagles has a clear purpose (e.g., territoriality, hunting), some flights, such as those by solitary birds or between well-established breeding pairs, seem to be play.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinguishing_from_other_species">Distinguishing from other species</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg/220px-Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg/330px-Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg/440px-Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_Flickr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Golden eagles are readily distinguished by their brown plumage, paler than most other <i>Aquila</i>, and pale nape patch</div></div></div>
<p>Size readily distinguishes this species from most other raptors when it is seen well. Most other raptors are considerably smaller. <i><a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">Buteo</a></i> hawks, which are perhaps most similar to the golden eagle in structure among the species outside of the "booted eagle" group, are often counted among the larger very common raptors. However, a mid-sized <i>Buteo</i> is dwarfed by a golden eagle, as an adult female eagle has about double the wingspan and about five times the weight. <i>Buteos</i> are also usually distinctly paler below, although some species occur in dark morphs which can be darker than a golden eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Among raptorial birds that share the golden eagle's range, only some <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">Old World vultures</a> and the <a href="/wiki/California_condor" title="California condor">California condor</a> are distinctly larger, with longer, broader wings, typically held more evenly in a slower, less forceful flight; they often have dramatically different colour patterns. In North America, the golden eagle may be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Turkey_vulture" title="Turkey vulture">turkey vulture</a> from a great distance, as it is a large species that, like the golden eagle, often flies with a pronounced dihedral. The turkey vulture can be distinguished by its less controlled, forceful flying style (they frequently rock back and forth unsteadily in even moderate winds) and its smaller, thinner body, much smaller head and, at closer range, its slaty black-brown colour and silvery wing secondaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunne_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Compared to <i><a href="/wiki/Haliaeetus" class="mw-redirect" title="Haliaeetus">Haliaeetus</a></i> eagles, the golden eagle has wings that are only somewhat more slender but are more <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawk</a>-like and lack the flat, plank-like wing positioning seen in the other genus.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunne_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Large northern <i>Haliaeetus</i> species usually have a larger bill and larger head which protrudes more distinctly than a golden eagle's in flight. The tail of the golden eagle is longer on average than those of <i>Haliaeetus</i> eagles, appearing to be two or three times the length of the head in soaring flight, whereas in the other eagles the head is often more than twice the length of the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunne_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Confusion is most likely between juvenile <i>Haliaeetus</i> and golden eagles, as the adult golden has a more solidly golden-brown coloration and all <i>Haliaeetus</i> eagles have obvious distinctive plumages as adults. <i>Haliaeetus</i> eagles are often heavily streaked in their juvenile phase. Juvenile golden eagles can have large patches of white on their wings and tail that are quite different from the random, sometimes large and splotchy-looking distribution of white typical of juvenile <i>Haliaeetus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dunne_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Distinguishing the golden eagle from other <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> eagles in Eurasia is more difficult. Identification may rely on the golden eagle's relatively long tail and patterns of white or grey on the wings and tail. Unlike golden eagles, other <i>Aquila</i> eagles do not generally fly in a pronounced dihedral. At close range, the golden to rufous nape-shawl of the golden eagle is distinctive from other <i>Aquila</i>. Most other <i>Aquila</i> eagles have darker plumage, although the smaller <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagle</a> is often paler than the golden eagle (the overlap in range is verified only in <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>). Among Eurasian <i>Aquila</i>, the adult <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagle</a> come closest to reaching the size of golden eagles, but both are distinguished by their longer necks, flatter wings in flight, white markings on their shoulder forewing-coverts, paler cream-straw coloured nape patch and generally darker colouration. Juvenile imperial eagles are much paler overall (caramel-cream in the Spanish; cream and tawny streaks in the eastern) and are not likely to be confused.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">Steppe eagles</a> can also approach golden eagles in size but are more compact and smaller headed with little colour variation to their dark earth-brown plumage, apart from juvenile birds which have distinctive cream-coloured bands running through their coverts and secondaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagles</a> are most similar in size and body shape to the golden, the body of the Verreaux's eagle being slightly longer overall but marginally less heavy and long-winged than the golden eagle's.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The plumage is very distinctly different, however, as Verreaux's eagles are almost entirely jet-black except for some striking, contrasting white on the wing primaries, shoulders and upper-wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This closely related species is known to co-occur with the golden eagle only in the <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Other booted eagles in the golden eagle's range are unlikely to be confused due to differences in size and form. The only species in the genus <i>Aquila</i> that exceeds the golden eagle in average wingspan and length is the <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">wedge-tailed eagle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>; however, the wedge-tailed eagle is a slightly less heavy bird.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_systematics">Taxonomy and systematics</span></h2>
<p>This <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his landmark 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a> as <i>Falco chrysaetos</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Since birds were grouped largely on superficial characteristics at that time, many species were grouped by Linnaeus into the genus <i>Falco</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Type_locality_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Type locality (biology)">type locality</a> was given simply as "Europa"; it was later fixed to <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>. It was moved to the new genus <i>Aquila</i> by French <a href="/wiki/Ornithologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornithologist">ornithologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-brisson_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brisson-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <i>Aquila</i> is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "eagle", possibly derived from <i>aquilus</i>, "dark in colour" and <i>chrysaetos</i> is <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> for the golden eagle from <i>khrusos</i>, "gold" and <i>aetos</i>, "eagle".<sup id="cite_ref-job90_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job90-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The golden eagle is part of a broad group of raptors called "booted eagles" which are defined by the feature that all species have feathering over their tarsus, unlike many other accipitrids which have bare legs. Included in this group are all species described as "hawk eagles" including the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Spizaetus" title="Spizaetus">Spizaetus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nisaetus" title="Nisaetus">Nisaetus</a></i>, as well as assorted <a href="/wiki/Monotypic_taxon" title="Monotypic taxon">monotypical genera</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Black-and-chestnut_eagle" title="Black-and-chestnut eagle">Oroaetus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Long-crested_eagle" title="Long-crested eagle">Lophaetus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crowned_eagle" title="Crowned eagle">Stephanoaetus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Martial_eagle" title="Martial eagle">Polemaetus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rufous-bellied_eagle" title="Rufous-bellied eagle">Lophotriorchis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Black_eagle" title="Black eagle">Ictinaetus</a></i>. The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> is distributed across every continent but for South America and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Up to 20 species have been classified in the genus, but more recently the taxonomic placement of some of the traditional species has been questioned. Traditionally, the <i>Aquila</i> eagles have been grouped superficially as largish, mainly brownish or dark-colored booted eagles that vary little in transition from their juvenile to their adult plumages. Genetic research has recently indicated the golden eagle is included in a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> with <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagle</a> in Africa as well as the <a href="/wiki/Gurney%27s_eagle" title="Gurney&#39;s eagle">Gurney's eagle</a> (<i>A. gurneyi</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">wedge-tailed eagle</a> (clearly part of an Australasian radiation of the lineage). This identification of this particular clade has long been suspected based on similar morphological characteristics amongst these large-bodied species.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> More surprisingly, the smaller, much paler-bellied sister species <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagle</a> (<i>A. fasciatus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a> (<i>A. spilogaster</i>), previously included in the genus <i>Hieraaetus</i>, have been revealed to be genetically much closer to the Verreaux's and golden eagle lineage than to other species traditionally included in the genus <i>Aquila</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Other largish <i>Aquila</i> species, the eastern imperial, the Spanish imperial, the tawny and the steppe eagles, are now thought to be separate, close-knit clade, which attained some similar characteristics to the prior clade via convergent evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Genetically, the "spotted eagles" (<i><a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">A. pomarina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_spotted_eagle" title="Indian spotted eagle">hastata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">clanga</a></i>), have been discovered to be more closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Long-crested_eagle" title="Long-crested eagle">long-crested eagle</a> (<i>Lophaetus occipitalis</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Black_eagle" title="Black eagle">black eagle</a> (<i>Ictinaetus malayensis</i>), and many generic reassignments have been advocated.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The genus <i>Hieraaetus</i>, including the <a href="/wiki/Booted_eagle" title="Booted eagle">booted eagle</a> (<i>H. pennatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Little_eagle" title="Little eagle">little eagle</a> (<i>H. morphnoides</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ayres%27s_hawk-eagle" title="Ayres&#39;s hawk-eagle">Ayres's hawk-eagle</a> (<i>H. ayresii</i>), consists of much smaller species, that are in fact smallest birds called eagles outside of the unrelated <i><a href="/wiki/Spilornis" title="Spilornis">Spilornis</a></i> serpent-eagle genus. This genus has recently been eliminated by many authorities and is now occasionally also included in <i>Aquila</i>, although not all ornithological unions have followed this suit in this re-classification.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The small-bodied <a href="/wiki/Wahlberg%27s_eagle" title="Wahlberg&#39;s eagle">Wahlberg's eagle</a> (<i>H. wahlbergi</i>) has been traditionally considered a <i>Aquila</i> species due to its lack of change from juvenile to adult plumage and brownish color but it is actually genetically aligned to the <i>Hieraaetus</i> lineage.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cassin%27s_hawk-eagle" title="Cassin&#39;s hawk-eagle">Cassin's hawk-eagle</a> (<i>H. africanus</i>) is also probably closely related to the <i>Hieraaetus</i> group rather than the <i>Spizaetus/Nisaetus</i> "hawk-eagle" group (in which it was previously classified) which is not known to have radiated to Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies_and_distribution">Subspecies and distribution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg/220px-Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg/330px-Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg/440px-Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4233" data-file-height="3175" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_RWD2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive <i>Aquila chrysaetos canadensis</i> shows the typical rusty coloration of the subspecies.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg/220px-Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg/330px-Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg/440px-Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5197" data-file-height="3780" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_homeyeri_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Aquila chrysaetos homeyeri</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are six extant <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of golden eagle that differ slightly in size and <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a>. Individuals of any of the subspecies are somewhat variable and the differences between the subspecies are <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal</a>, especially in terms of body size. Other than these characteristics, there is little variation across the range of the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Some recent studies have gone so far as to propose that only two subspecies be recognized based on genetic markers: <i>Aquila chrysaetos chrysaetos</i> (including <i>A. c. homeyeri</i>) and <i>A. c. canadensis</i> (including <i>A. c. japonica</i>, <i>A. c. daphanea</i> and <i>A. c. kamtschatica</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="anchor" id="chrysaetos"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos chrysaetos</i> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">1758</a>)</small> – sometimes referred to as the <b>European golden eagle</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This is the <a href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominate subspecies">nominate subspecies</a>. This subspecies is found almost throughout Europe, including the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> (mainly in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>), a lion's share of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, southern and northernmost <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, it is found from <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> in southeastern Europe. It is also distributed through <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, reportedly reaching its eastern limit around the <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a> in Russia, also ranging south at a similar <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> into western <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Male wing length is from 56.5 to 67&#160;cm (22.2 to 26.4&#160;in), averaging 62&#160;cm (24&#160;in), and female wing length is from 61.5 to 71.2&#160;cm (24.2 to 28.0&#160;in), averaging 67&#160;cm (26&#160;in). Males weigh from 2.8 to 4.6&#160;kg (6.2 to 10.1&#160;lb), averaging 3.69&#160;kg (8.1&#160;lb), and females weigh from 3.8 to 6.7&#160;kg (8.4 to 14.8&#160;lb), averaging 5.17&#160;kg (11.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The male of this subspecies has a wingspan of 1.89 to 2.15&#160;m (6&#160;ft 2&#160;in to 7&#160;ft 1&#160;in), with an average of 2.02&#160;m (6&#160;ft 8&#160;in), with the female's typical wingspan range is 2.12 to 2.2&#160;m (6&#160;ft 11&#160;in to 7&#160;ft 3&#160;in), with an average of 2.16&#160;m (7&#160;ft 1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This is a medium-sized subspecies and is the palest. As opposed to golden eagles found further east in Eurasia, the adults of this subspecies are a tawny golden-brown on the upperside. The nape patch is often gleaming golden in color and the feathers here are exceptionally long.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="homeyeri"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos homeyeri</i> <small>Severtzov, 1888</small> – commonly known as the <b>Iberian golden eagle</b>. This subspecies occurs in almost the entirety of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> as well as the island of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, though it is absent from the rest of continental Europe. It also ranges in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> in a narrow sub-coastal strip from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. A completely isolated population of golden eagles is found in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a>, at the southern limit of the species' range worldwide. Although this latter population has not been formally assigned to a subspecies, there is a high probability that it belongs with <i>A. c. homeyeri</i>. This subspecies also ranges in much of <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, mainly <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, spottily through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> into northern <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> to its eastern limits throughout the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> much of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and north to southwestern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Male wing length is from 55 to 64.3&#160;cm (21.7 to 25.3&#160;in), averaging 59&#160;cm (23&#160;in), and female wing length is from 60 to 70.5&#160;cm (23.6 to 27.8&#160;in), averaging 64&#160;cm (25&#160;in). Weight is from 2.9 to 6&#160;kg (6.4 to 13.2&#160;lb) with no known reports of average masses.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is slightly smaller and darker plumaged than the nominate subspecies, but it is not as dark as the golden eagles found further to the east. The forehead and crown are dark brownish, with the nape patch being short-feathered and a relatively light rusty color.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="daphanea"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos daphanea</i> <small>Severtzov, 1888</small> – known variously as the <b>Asian golden eagle</b>, <b>Himalayan golden eagle</b> or <b>berkut</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is distributed in central <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and the easternmost <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, distributed to <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and central <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and along the Himalayas from northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> and discontinuing in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> (rarely ranging over into northernmost <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pcr_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcr-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is the largest on average. Male wing length is from 60 to 68&#160;cm (24 to 27&#160;in), averaging 64&#160;cm (25&#160;in), and female wing length is from 66 to 72&#160;cm (26 to 28&#160;in), averaging 70&#160;cm (28&#160;in). No range of body weights are known, but males will weigh approximately 4.05&#160;kg (8.9&#160;lb) and females 6.35&#160;kg (14.0&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Although the wingspan of this subspecies reportedly averages 2.21&#160;m (7&#160;ft 3&#160;in), some individuals can have much longer wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> One female berkut had an authenticated wingspan of 2.81&#160;m (9&#160;ft 3&#160;in), although she was a captive specimen.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is generally the second-darkest subspecies, being blackish on the back. The forehead and crown are dark with a blackish cap near the end of the crown. The feathers of the nape and top-neck are rich brown-red. The nape feathers are slightly shorter than in the nominate subspecies and are similar in length to <i>A. c. homeyeri</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Indian_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indian-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="japonica"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos japonica</i> <small>Severtzov, 1888</small> – commonly known as the <b>Japanese golden eagle</b>. This subspecies is found in northern <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (the islands of <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> and discontinuously in <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>) and undefined parts of <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Male wing length is from 58 to 59.5&#160;cm (22.8 to 23.4&#160;in), averaging 59&#160;cm (23&#160;in), and female wing length is from 62 to 64.5&#160;cm (24.4 to 25.4&#160;in), averaging 63&#160;cm (25&#160;in). No range of body weights are known, but males will weigh approximately 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb) and females 3.25&#160;kg (7.2&#160;lb). This is, by far, the smallest-bodied subspecies. It is also the darkest, with even adults being a slaty-grayish black on the back and crown and juveniles being similar, but with darker black plumage contrasting with brownish color and white scaling on the wings, flank and tail. This subspecies has bright rufous nape feathers that are quite loose and long. Adult Japanese golden eagles often maintain extensive white mottling on the inner-webs of the tail that tend to be more typical of juvenile eagles in other subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="canadensis"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos canadensis</i> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">1758</a>)</small> – commonly known as the <b>American golden eagle</b>. Occupies the species' entire range in North America, which comprises the great majority of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a>. The species is found breeding occasionally in all Canadian provinces but for <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>. It is currently absent in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a> as breeding species east of a line from <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> down through westernmost <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> to <a href="/wiki/West_Texas" title="West Texas">West Texas</a>. The southern limits of its range are in central Mexico, from the <a href="/wiki/Guadalajara" title="Guadalajara">Guadalajara</a> area in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Tampico" title="Tampico">Tampico</a> area in the east; it is the "Mexican eagle" featured on the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Mexico" title="Coat of arms of Mexico">coat of arms of Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> It is the subspecies with the largest breeding range and is probably the most numerous subspecies, especially if <i>A. c. kamtschatica</i> is included.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Male wing length is from 59.1 to 64&#160;cm (23.3 to 25.2&#160;in), averaging 61&#160;cm (24&#160;in), and female wing length is from 60.1 to 67.4&#160;cm (23.7 to 26.5&#160;in), averaging 65&#160;cm (26&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bortolotti_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bortolotti-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The average wingspan in both sexes is about 2.04&#160;m (6&#160;ft 8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Males weigh from 2.5 to 4.47&#160;kg (5.5 to 9.9&#160;lb), averaging 3.48&#160;kg (7.7&#160;lb), and females typically weigh from 3.6 to 6.4&#160;kg (7.9 to 14.1&#160;lb), averaging 4.91&#160;kg (10.8&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The subspecies does not appear to follow <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> (the rule that widely distributed organisms are larger-bodied further away from the Equator), as specimens of both sexes from <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> had a mean weight of 4.22&#160;kg (9.3&#160;lb) and where slightly heavier than those from <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, with a mean weight of 3.76&#160;kg (8.3&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It is medium-sized, being generally intermediate in size between the nominate and <i>A. c. homeyeri</i>, but with much overlap. It is blackish to dark brown on the back. The long feathers of the nape and top-neck are rusty-reddish and slightly narrower and darker than in the nominate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell2_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell2-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="kamtschatica"></span> <i>Aquila chrysaetos kamtschatica</i> <small><a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Severtzov" title="Nikolai Severtzov">Severtzov</a>, 1888</small> – sometimes referred to as the <b>Siberian golden eagle</b> or the <b>Kamchatkan golden eagle</b>. This subspecies ranges from Western Siberia (where overlap with <i>A. c. chrysaetos</i> is probable), across most of Russia, including the <a href="/wiki/Altay_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Altay Mountains">Altay</a> (spilling over into Northern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>), to the <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka_Peninsula" title="Kamchatka Peninsula">Kamchatka Peninsula</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anadyrsky_District" title="Anadyrsky District">Anadyrsky District</a>. This subspecies is often included in <i>A. c. canadensis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Male wing length is from 61.8 to 70.5&#160;cm (24.3 to 27.8&#160;in), averaging 64&#160;cm (25&#160;in), and female wing length is from 65 to 72&#160;cm (26 to 28&#160;in), averaging 69&#160;cm (27&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> No weights are known in this subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The coloration of these eagles is almost exactly the same as in <i>A. c. canadensis</i>. The main difference is that this subspecies is much larger in size, being nearly the equal of <i>A. c. daphanea</i> if going on wing-length.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><span class="anchor" id="bonifacti"></span>The larger <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a> golden eagles of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (and possibly elsewhere) are referred to a <a href="/wiki/Paleosubspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleosubspecies">paleosubspecies</a> <i>Aquila chrysaetos bonifacti</i>, and the huge specimens of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> of Liko Cave (<a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>) have been named <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_chrysaetos_simurgh" title="Aquila chrysaetos simurgh">Aquila chrysaetos simurgh</a></i> (Weesie, 1988).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, an ancestral golden eagle, with a heavier, broader skull, larger wings and shorter legs when compared to modern birds, has been found in the <a href="/wiki/La_Brea_Tar_Pits" title="La Brea Tar Pits">La Brea Tar Pits</a> of southern <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_and_distribution">Habitat and distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg/220px-North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg/330px-North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg/440px-North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="437" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_east_ridge_of_Beinn_Mhor._-_geograph.org.uk_-_15940.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ben_More_(Mull)" title="Ben More (Mull)">Beinn Mhor</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Mull" title="Isle of Mull">Isle of Mull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> is typical golden eagle habitat: rugged and mountainous.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg/220px-Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg/330px-Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg/440px-Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Golden_Eagle_from_Singba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary_in_North_Sikkim_India_02042019.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult flying above Himalayan mountains from <a href="/wiki/Shingba_Rhododendron_Sanctuary" title="Shingba Rhododendron Sanctuary">Shingba Rhododendron Sanctuary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Golden eagles are fairly adaptable in habitat but often reside in areas with a few shared ecological characteristics. They are best suited to hunting in open or semi-open areas and search them out year-around. Native vegetation seems to be attractive to them and they typically avoid developed areas of any type from urban to agricultural as well as heavily forested regions. In desolate areas (e.g., the southern <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>), they can occur regularly at roadkills and garbage dumps.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The largest numbers of golden eagles are found in mountainous regions today, with many eagles doing a majority of their hunting and nesting on rock formations. However, they are not solely tied to high elevations and can breed in lowlands if the local habitats are suitable. Below are more detailed description of habitats occupied by golden eagles in both continents where they occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasia">Eurasia</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> fringe of Eurasia, golden eagles occur along the edge of the <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kola_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Kola peninsula">Kola peninsula</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chukotka_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Chukotka Autonomous Okrug">Anadyr</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, nesting in forests and hunting over nearby arctic <a href="/wiki/Heath" title="Heath">heathland</a>. Typical vegetation is stunted, fragmented <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch woodland</a> merging into low <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a>-<a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a> scrub and various heathland. In the rocky, wet, windy maritime climate of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, the golden eagle dwells in mountains. These areas include upland <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grasslands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blanket_bog" title="Blanket bog">blanket bog</a>, and sub-Arctic heaths but also fragmented woodland and <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">woodland edge</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">boreal forests</a>. In Western Europe, golden eagle habitat is dominated by open, rough grassland, heath and bogs, and rocky ridges, spurs, <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">crags</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scree" title="Scree">scree</a>, slopes and grand plateaux. In <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic States">Baltic States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and almost the entire distribution in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> all the way to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, golden eagles occur sparsely in lowland <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> forest. These areas are dominated by stands of evergreens such as <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larch" title="Larch">larch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>, occasionally supplemented by <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> stands in southern Scandinavia and the Baltic States. This is largely marginal country for golden eagles and they occur where tree cover is thin and abuts open habitat. Golden eagle taiga habitat usually consists of extensive peatland formations caused by poorly drained soils. In central Europe, golden eagles today occur almost exclusively in the major mountain ranges, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carpathians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpathians">Carpathians</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>. Here, the species nests near the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> and hunt <a href="/wiki/Transhumance_in_the_Alps" class="mw-redirect" title="Transhumance in the Alps">subalpine and alpine pastures</a>, grassland and heath above. Golden eagles also occur in moderately mountainous habitat along the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>. This area is characterized by low mountains, Mediterranean <a href="/wiki/Maquis_shrubland" title="Maquis shrubland">maquis vegetation</a>, and sub-temperate open woodland. The local <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a>-<a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> vegetation, with a variety of <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">Sclerophyllous</a> shrubs are well-adapted to prolonged summer droughts. From <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a> and the southern <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> to the foothills of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, the typical golden eagle habitat is temperate desert-like mountain ranges surrounded by steppe landscapes interspersed with forest. Here the climate is colder and more continental than around the Mediterranean. Golden eagles occupy the alpine ranges from the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, in the great <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayan massif</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, where they occupy the <a href="/wiki/Tien_Shan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tien Shan">Tien Shan</a> range.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In these mountain ranges, the species often lives at very high elevations, living above tree line at more than 2,500&#160;m (8,200&#160;ft), often nesting in rocky scree and hunting in adjacent meadows. In Tibet, golden eagles inhabit high ridges and passes in the <a href="/wiki/Lhasa" title="Lhasa">Lhasa River watershed</a>, where they regularly joins groups of soaring <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_vulture" title="Himalayan vulture">Himalayan vultures</a> (<i>Gyps himalayensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> One golden eagle was recorded circling at 6,190&#160;m (20,310&#160;ft) above sea-level in <a href="/wiki/Khumbu" title="Khumbu">Khumbu</a> in May 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In the mountains of Japan and Korea, the golden eagle occupies deciduous scrub woodland and carpet-like stands of <a href="/wiki/Siberian_dwarf_pine" class="mw-redirect" title="Siberian dwarf pine">Siberian dwarf pine</a> (<i>Pinus pumila</i>) that merge into grasslands and alpine heathland. The golden eagle occurs in mountains from the <a href="/wiki/Adrar_Plateau" title="Adrar Plateau">Adrar Plateau</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> to northern <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> habitat is largely bereft of vegetation but offers many rocky plateaus to support both the eagles and their prey. In <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, their habitat is mainly rocky slopes and wide <a href="/wiki/Wadi" title="Wadi">wadi</a> areas, chiefly in desert and to a lesser extent in semi-desert and Mediterranean climates, extending to open areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Shirhai_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirhai-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northeastern Africa">Northeastern Africa</a>, the habitat is often of a sparse, desert-like character and is quite similar to the habitat in Middle East and the Arabian peninsula. In Ethiopia's <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a>, where the vegetation is more lush and the climate is clearly less arid than in Northeastern Africa, the golden eagle occupies verdant mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a> occupied by golden eagles are roughly concurrent with those of Eurasia. In western and northern <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ungava_Peninsula" title="Ungava Peninsula">Ungava Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, the eagles occupy the Arctic fringe of North America (the species does not range into the true high Arctic tundra), where open canopy gives way to dwarf-shrub heathland with <a href="/wiki/Eriophorum" title="Eriophorum">cottongrass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tussock_(grass)" title="Tussock (grass)">tussock</a> tundra. In land-locked areas of the sub-Arctic, golden eagles are by far the largest raptor. From the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Range" title="Alaska Range">Alaska Range</a> to <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, it is often found in high mountains above the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> or on bluffs and cliffs along river valleys below the tree line.<sup id="cite_ref-Ritchie_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petersen_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petersen-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In Washington state, golden eagles can be found in clear-cut sections of otherwise dense coniferous forest zones with relatively little annual precipitation.<sup id="cite_ref-Marr_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marr-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> From east of the Canadian Rocky Mountains to the mountains of Labrador, the golden eagle is found in small numbers in boreal forest <a href="/wiki/Peatland" class="mw-redirect" title="Peatland">peatlands</a> and similar <a href="/wiki/Mixed_Wood_Plains_Ecozone_(CEC)" title="Mixed Wood Plains Ecozone (CEC)">mixed woodland areas</a>. In the foothills of the Rocky Mountains in the United States are <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairies</a> where golden eagles are widespread, especially where there's a low human presence. Here, grassland on low rolling hills and flat plains are typical, interrupted only by <a href="/wiki/Populus_sect._Aigeiros" title="Populus sect. Aigeiros">cottonwood stands</a> around river valleys and <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> where the eagles may build their nests. Golden eagles also occupy the desert-like <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a> from southern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> to northern <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>. In this habitat, trees are generally absent other than <a href="/wiki/Juniper" title="Juniper">junipers</a> with vegetation being dominated by <a href="/wiki/Artemisia_(genus)" title="Artemisia (genus)">sagebrush</a> (<i>Artemisia</i>) and other low shrub species. Although the vegetation varies a bit more, similar habitat is occupied by golden eagles in Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-Craig84_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig84-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-marz_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marz-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> However, golden eagles are typically absent in North America from true deserts, like the <a href="/wiki/Sonora_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonora Desert">Sonora Desert</a>, where annual precipitation is less than 20&#160;cm (7.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles occupy the mountains and coastal areas of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baja_California" title="Baja California">Baja California</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> where hot, dry summers and moist winters are typical. The golden eagles here often nest in <a href="/wiki/Chaparral" title="Chaparral">chaparral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> woodland, oak savanna and grassland amongst low rolling hill typified by diverse vegetation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In the Eastern United States, the species once bred widely in the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Plateau" title="Appalachian Plateau">Appalachian Plateau</a> near <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">burns</a>, open <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meadow" title="Meadow">meadows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spottford_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spottford-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In Eastern North America, the species still breeds on the <a href="/wiki/Gaspe_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaspe Peninsula">Gaspe Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>.
