Until 1999, a pair of golden eagles were still known to nest in <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> but they are now believed to be absent as a breeding bird from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The golden eagles who breed in eastern Canada winter on montane grass and heath fields in the Appalachian Plateau region, especially in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. Most sightings in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">Eastern United States</a> recently are concentrated within or along southwestern border of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Plateau" title="Appalachian Plateau">Appalachian Plateau</a> (30% of records) and within the Coastal Plain physiographic region (33% of records).<sup id="cite_ref-Millaps_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millaps-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Though they do regularly nest in the marsh-like <a href="/wiki/Mire" title="Mire">peatland</a> of the boreal forest, golden eagles are not generally associated with wetlands and, in fact, they can be found near some of the most arid spots on earth. In the wintering population of Eastern United States, however, they are often associated with steep river valleys, reservoirs, and marshes in inland areas as well as estuarine marshlands, barrier islands, managed wetlands, sounds, and mouths of major river systems in coastal areas. These wetlands are attractive due to a dominance of open vegetation, large concentrations of prey, and the general absence of human disturbance.<sup id="cite_ref-Millaps_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millaps-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In the midwestern United States, they are not uncommon during winter near <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a> and wildlife refuges that provide foraging opportunities at waterfowl concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dietary_biology_of_the_golden_eagle" title="Dietary biology of the golden eagle">Dietary biology of the golden eagle</a></div>
<p>Golden eagles usually hunt during daylight hours, but were recorded hunting from one hour before <a href="/wiki/Sunrise" title="Sunrise">sunrise</a> to one hour after <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">sunset</a> during the breeding season in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The hunting success rate of golden eagles was calculated in Idaho, showing that, out of 115 hunting attempts, 20% were successful in procuring prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Tandem_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tandem-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> A fully-grown golden eagle requires about 230 to 250&#160;g (8.1 to 8.8&#160;oz) of food per day but in the life of most eagles there are cycles of feast and famine, and eagles have been known to go without food for up to a week and then gorge on up to 900&#160;g (2.0&#160;lb) at one sitting.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Activity_and_movements">Activity and movements</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg/220px-Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg/330px-Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg/440px-Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2695" data-file-height="2695" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_La_Canada_2012-01-14.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, golden eagles such as this one in the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_%C3%81vila" title="Province of Ávila">Province of Ávila</a> are sedentary.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the dramatic ways in which they attain food and interact with raptors of their own and other species, the daily life of golden eagles is often rather uneventful. In <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, adult male golden eagles were observed to sit awake on a perch for an average of 78% of daylight, whereas adult females sat on nest or perched for an average of 85% of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Collopy2_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collopy2-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> During the peak of summer in <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, hunting and territorial flights occurred mostly between 9:00 and 11:00 am and 4:00 and 6:00 pm, with the remaining 15 or so hours of daylight spent perching or resting.<sup id="cite_ref-Leslie_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leslie-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When conditions are heavily <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anticyclonic</a>, there is less soaring during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> During winter in Scotland, golden eagles soar frequently in order to scan the environment for carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the more wooded environments of Norway during autumn and winter, much less aerial activity is reported, since the eagles tend to avoid detection by actively contour-hunting rather than looking for carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunnar_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles are believed to sleep through much of the night. Although usually highly solitary outside of the bond between breeding pairs, exceptionally cold weather in winter may cause eagles to put their usual guard down and perch together. The largest known congregation of golden eagles was observed on an extremely cold winter's night in eastern Idaho when 124 individuals were observed perched closely along a line of 85 power poles.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span></h3>
<p>Most populations of golden eagles are sedentary, but the species is actually a <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">partial migrant</a>. Golden eagles are very hardy species, being well adapted to cold climates, however they cannot abide declining available food sources in the northern stretches of their range. Eagles raised at latitudes greater than 60° N are usually migratory, though a short migration may be untaken by those who breed or hatch at about 50° N.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> During migration, they often use soaring-gliding flight, rather than powered flight.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, most banded juveniles move between 1,000 and 2,000&#160;km (620 and 1,240&#160;mi) due south, whereas adults stay locally through winter. Further east, conditions are too harsh for even wintering territorial adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles that breed from the <a href="/wiki/Kola_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Kola peninsula">Kola peninsula</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chukotka_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Chukotka Autonomous Okrug">Anadyr</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a> migrate south to winter on the Russian and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolian</a> steppes, and the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plains</a>. The flat, relatively open landscapes in these regions hold relatively few resident breeding golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Similarly the entire population of golden eagles from northern and central Alaska and northern Canada migrates south. At <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lorette" title="Mount Lorette">Mount Lorette</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, approximately 4,000 golden eagles may pass during the fall, the largest recorded migration of golden eagles on earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherrington_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrington-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Here the mountain ranges are relatively moderate and consistent, thus being reliable for thermals and updrafts which made long-distance migrating feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherrington_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrington-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Birds hatched in <a href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Denali National Park">Denali National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> traveled from 818 to 4,815&#160;km (508 to 2,992&#160;mi) to their winter ranges in western North America.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> These western migrants may winter anywhere from southern <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a> to <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> and from inland <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>. Adults who bred in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> reached their wintering grounds, which range from central <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> to southern <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> to northeastern <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, in 26 to 40 days, with arrival dates from November to early December.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The departure dates from wintering grounds are variable. In southwestern Canada, they leave their wintering grounds by 6 April to 8 May (the mean being 21 April); in southwestern Idaho, wintering birds leave from 20 March to 13 April (mean of 29 March); and in the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwestern United States</a>, wintering birds may depart by early March.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in the species' breeding range, golden eagles (i.e., those who breed in the contiguous Western United States, all of Europe but for Northern Scandinavia, North Africa and all of Asia but for Northern Russia) are non-migratory and tend to remain within striking distance of their breeding territories throughout the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In Scotland, among all recovered, banded golden eagles (36 out of 1000, the rest mostly died or disappeared) the average distance between ringing and recovery was 44&#160;km (27&#160;mi), averaging 63&#160;km (39&#160;mi) in juveniles and 36&#160;km (22&#160;mi) in older birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the dry <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwestern United States</a>, golden eagles tend to move to higher elevations once the breeding season is complete.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, populations breeding at lower latitudes, like Morocco, are mostly sedentary, although some occasionally disperse after breeding to areas outside of the normal breeding range.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territoriality">Territoriality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BK1D0090.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BK1D0090.jpg/220px-BK1D0090.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BK1D0090.jpg/330px-BK1D0090.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BK1D0090.jpg/440px-BK1D0090.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1998" data-file-height="1332" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BK1D0090.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two golden eagles in an aerial conflict over their home ranges, the upper bird clearly a juvenile.</div></div></div>
<p>Territoriality is believed to be the primary cause of interactions and confrontations between non-paired golden eagles. Golden eagles maintain some of the largest known home ranges (or territories) of any bird species but there is much variation of home range size across the range, possibly dictated by food abundance and habitat preference. Home ranges in most of the range can vary from 20 to 200&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (7.7 to 77.2&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_County" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego County">San Diego County</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, the home ranges varied from 49 to 137&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (19 to 53&#160;sq&#160;mi), with an average of 93&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (36&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> However, some home ranges have been much smaller, such as in southwestern Idaho where, possibly due to an abundance of jackrabbits, home ranges as small as 4.85&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1.87&#160;sq&#160;mi) are maintained.<sup id="cite_ref-marz_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marz-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The smallest known home ranges on record for golden eagles are in the Bale Mountains of Ethiopia, where they range from 1.5 to 9&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (0.58 to 3.47&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Clouet_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clouet-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> 46% of undulating displays in Montana occurred shortly after the juvenile eagles left their parents range, suggesting that some residents defend and maintain territories year-round.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere it is stated that home ranges are less strictly maintained during winter but hunting grounds are basically exclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In Israel and Scotland, aggressive encounters peaked from winter until just before egg-laying and were less common during the nesting season.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bahat_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bahat-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Threat displays include undulating flight and aggressive direct flapping flight with exaggerated downstrokes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Most displays by mature golden eagles (67% for males and 76% for females) occur, rather than around the nest, at the edge of their home ranges.<sup id="cite_ref-Collopy2_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collopy2-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In Western Norway, most recorded undulating flight displays occur during the pre-laying period in late winter/early spring.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunnar_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Display flights seem to be triggered by the presence of other golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The use of display flights has a clear benefit in that it lessens the need for physical confrontations, which can be fatal.<sup id="cite_ref-Collopy2_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collopy2-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Usually, non-breeding birds are treated aggressively by the golden eagle maintaining their home range, normally being chased to the apparent limit of the range but with no actual physical contact.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The territorial flight of the adult golden eagle is sometimes preceded or followed by intense bouts of undulating displays. The invader often responds by rolling over and presenting talons to the aggressor. Rarely, the two eagles will lock talons and tumble through the air; sometimes fall several revolutions and in some cases even tumble to the ground before releasing their grip.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunnar_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunnar-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of the Alps, the golden eagle population has reached the saturation point in appropriate habitat and apparently violent confrontations are more common than in other parts of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles may express their aggression via body language while perched, typically the adult female when confronted by an intruding eagle: the head and body are upright, feathers on head and neck are erect; the wings may be slightly spread and beak open; often accompanied by intense gaze. They then often engage in a similar posture with wings spread wide and oriented toward the threat; sometimes rocking back on tail and even flopping over onto the back with talons extended upward as defense. Such behavior may be accompanied by wing slap against the threatening intruder.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> When approached by an intruder, the defending eagle turns away, partially spreads tail, lowers head, and remains still; adults on the nest may lower head and "freeze" when approached by a person or a helicopter.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Females in Israel displayed more than males and mostly against interspecific intruders; males apparently displayed primarily as part of courtship.<sup id="cite_ref-Bahat_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bahat-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Five of 7 aggressive encounters at carcasses during winter in Norway were won by females; in 15 of 21 conflicts, the younger bird dominated an older conspecific.<sup id="cite_ref-Halley_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halley-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> However, obvious juvenile eagles (apparent to the adult eagles due to the amount of white on their wings and tail) are sometimes allowed to penetrate deeply into a pair’s home range and all parties commonly ignore each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Kochert_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kochert-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, it was verified that parent eagles were not aggressive towards their own young after the nesting period and some juveniles stayed on their parents territory until their 2nd spring and then left by their own accord.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Toole_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Toole-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproduction_and_life_cycle_of_the_golden_eagle" title="Reproduction and life cycle of the golden eagle">Reproduction and life cycle of the golden eagle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG/220px-Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG/330px-Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG/440px-Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nid_d%27Aigle.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">Eyrie</a> (in hollow at left center) in the Valley of the <a href="/wiki/Siagne" title="Siagne">Siagne de la Pare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpes-Maritimes" title="Alpes-Maritimes">Alpes-Maritimes</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg/220px-Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg/330px-Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg/440px-Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.88.3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Aquila chrysaetos</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<p>Golden eagles usually mate for life. A breeding pair is formed in a courtship display. This courtship includes undulating displays by both in the pair, with the male bird picking up a piece of rock or a small stick, and dropping it only to enter into a steep dive and catch it in mid-air, repeating the maneuver 3 or more times. The female takes a clump of earth and drops and catches it in the same fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cornell2_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell2-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Golden eagles typically build several <a href="/wiki/Eyrie_(nest)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyrie (nest)">eyries</a> within their territory (preferring cliffs) and use them alternately for several years. Their nesting areas are characterized by the extreme regularity of the nest spacing.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Mating and egg-laying timing for golden eagle is variable depending on the locality. Copulation normally lasts 10–20 seconds. Mating seems to occur around 40–46 days before the initial egg-laying.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The golden eagle chick may be heard from within the egg 15 hours before it begins hatching. After the first chip is broken off of the egg, there is no activity for around 27 hours. After this period, the hatching activity accelerates and the shell is broken apart in 35 hours. The chick is completely free in 37 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the first 10 days, chicks mainly lie down on the nest substrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The eagles are capable of preening on their second day but are continually thermoregulated via brooding by their parents until around 20 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Within 10 days, the hatchlings grow considerably, weighing around 500&#160;g (1.1&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Collopy3_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collopy3-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Around this age, they also start sitting up more.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Around 20 days of age, the chicks generally start standing, which becomes the main position over the course of the next 40 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis2_25-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The whitish down continues until around 25 days of age, at which point it is gradually replaced by dark <a href="/wiki/Pennaceous_feather" title="Pennaceous feather">contour feathers</a> that eclipse the down and the birds attain a general <a href="/wiki/Piebald" title="Piebald">piebald</a> appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> After hatching, 80% of food items and 90% of food biomass is captured and brought to the nest by the adult male. Fledging occurs at 66 to 75 days of age in Idaho and 70 to 81 days in Scotland. The first attempted flight departure after fledging can be abrupt, with the young jumping off and using a series of short, stiff wing-beats to glide downward or being blown out of nest while wing-flapping. 18 to 20 days after first fledging, the young eagles will take their first circling flight, but they cannot gain height as efficiently as their parents until approximately 60 days after fledging. In <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>, young golden eagles were first seen hunting large prey 59 days after fledging. 75 to 85 days after fledging, the young were largely independent of parents. Generally, breeding success seems to be greatest where prey is available in abundance.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Longevity">Longevity</span></h2>
<p>Golden eagles are fairly long-living birds in natural conditions. The survival rate of raptorial birds tends to increase with larger body size, with a 30–50% annual loss of population rate in small falcons/accipiters, a 15–25% loss of population rate in medium-sized hawks (e.g., <i>Buteo</i>s or kites) and a 5% or less rate of loss in eagles and vultures. The oldest known wild golden eagle was a bird banded in Sweden which was recovered 32 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The longest-lived known wild golden eagle in North America was 31 years and 8 months.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The longest-lived known captive golden eagle, a specimen in Europe, survived to 46 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The estimated adult annual survival rate on <a href="/wiki/Skye" class="mw-redirect" title="Skye">the Isle of Skye</a> in Scotland is around 97.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> When this extrapolated into an estimated lifespan this results in 39 and half years as the average for adult golden eagles in this area, which is probably far too high an estimate.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Survival rates are usually much lower in juvenile eagles than in adult eagles. In the western <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, 50% of golden eagles banded in the nest died by the time they were 2 and a half years and an estimated 75% died by the time they were 5 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Near a wind turbine facility in west-central California, estimated survival rates, based on conventional telemetry of 257 individuals, were 84% for first-year eagles, 79% for 1- to 3-year-olds and adult floaters and 91% for breeders; with no difference in survival rates between sexes.<sup id="cite_ref-Hunt_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Survival rates may be lower for migrating populations of golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre2_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre2-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> A 19–34% survival rate was estimated for juvenile eagles from <a href="/wiki/Denali_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Denali National Park">Denali National Park</a> in their first 11 months.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre2_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre2-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The average life expectancy of golden eagles in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> is 13 years, extrapolated from a reported mere 92.5% survival rate.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_mortality">Natural mortality</span></h3>
<p>Natural sources of mortality are largely reported in anecdotes. On rare occasions, golden eagles have been killed by competing predators or by hunting mammalian carnivores, including the aforementioned wolverine, snow leopard, cougar, brown bear and white-tailed eagle attacks. Most competitive attacks resulting in death probably occur at the talons of other golden eagles. Nestlings and fledglings are more likely to be killed by another predator than free-flying juveniles and adults. It has been suspected that golden eagle nests may be predated more frequently by other predators (especially birds, which are often the only other large animals that can access a golden eagle nest without the assistance of man-made climbing equipment) in areas where golden eagles are regularly disturbed at the nest by humans. Jeff Watson believed that common raven occasionally eats golden eagle eggs but only in situations where the parent eagles have abandoned their nesting attempt.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, there are no confirmed accounts of predation by other bird species on golden eagle nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, golden eagles may be killed by their prey in self-defense. There is an account of a golden eagle dying from the quills of a <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">North American porcupine</a> (<i>Erethizon dorsatum</i>) it had attempted to hunt.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B9m" title="Rùm">Isle of Rùm</a> in Scotland, there are a few cases of red deer trampling golden eagles to death, probably the result of a doe having intercepted a bird that was trying to kill a fawn.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  Although usually well out-matched by the predator, occasionally other large birds can put up a formidable fight against a golden eagle.  An attempted capture of a <a href="/wiki/Great_blue_heron" title="Great blue heron">great blue heron</a> by a golden eagle resulted in the death of both birds from wounds sustained in the ensuing fight.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>  There is at least one case in Scotland of a golden eagle dying after being "oiled" by a <a href="/wiki/Northern_fulmar" title="Northern fulmar">northern fulmar</a>, a bird whose primary defense against predators is to disgorge an oily secretion which may inhibit the predator's ability to fly.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>  Of natural sources of death, starvation is probably under-reported.  11 of 16 dead juvenile eagles which had hatched in Denali National Park had died of starvation.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntyre2_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntyre2-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>  Of 36 deaths of golden eagles in Idaho, 55% were possibly attributable to natural causes, specifically 8 (26%) from unknown trauma, 3 (10%) from disease and 6 (19%) from unknown causes.<sup id="cite_ref-Bortolotti2_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bortolotti2-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>  Of 266 golden eagle deaths in Spain, only 6% were from unknown causes that could not be directly attributed to human activities.<sup id="cite_ref-Arroyo_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arroyo-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  Avian cholera caused by bacteria (<i><a href="/wiki/Pasteurella_multocida" title="Pasteurella multocida">Pasteurella multocida</a></i>) infects eagles that eat waterfowl that have died from the disease.  The <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoan</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Trichomonas" title="Trichomonas">Trichomonas</a></i> sp. caused the deaths of four fledglings in a study of wild golden eagles in Idaho.<sup id="cite_ref-Beecham_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beecham-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Several further diseases that contribute to golden eagle deaths have been examined in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A captive eagle died from two <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">malignant tumors</a> – one in the liver and one in the kidney.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Killing_permits">Killing permits</span></h2>
<p>In December 2016, the <a href="/wiki/US_Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="US Fish and Wildlife Service">US Fish and Wildlife Service</a> proposed allowing wind-turbine electric generation companies to kill golden eagles without penalty, so long as "companies take steps to minimize the losses".  If issued, the permits would last 30 years, six times the current 5-year permits.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_human_culture">In human culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagles_in_human_culture" title="Golden eagles in human culture">Golden eagles in human culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg/220px-Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg/330px-Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg/440px-Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1773" data-file-height="1093" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunting_with_Golden_Eagles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1870s illustration of <i>burkut</i> <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mankind has been fascinated by the golden eagle as early as the beginning of recorded history. Most early-recorded cultures regarded the golden eagle with reverence. It was only after the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, when sport-hunting became widespread and commercial stock farming became internationally common, that humans started to widely regard golden eagles as a threat to their livelihoods. This period also brought about the firearm and industrialized poisons, which made it easy for humans to kill the evasive and powerful birds.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_and_conservation">Status and conservation</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Status_and_conservation_of_the_golden_eagle" title="Status and conservation of the golden eagle">Status and conservation of the golden eagle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_3_(Martin_Mecnarowski).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/220px-Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/330px-Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg/440px-Aquila_chrysaetos_3_%28Martin_Mecnarowski%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_chrysaetos_3_(Martin_Mecnarowski).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In flight in <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a></div></div></div>
<p>At one time, the golden eagle lived in a great majority of temperate <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, North <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, North <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Although widespread and quite secure in some areas, in many parts of the range golden eagles have experienced sharp population declines and have even been extirpated from some areas.  The number of golden eagles from around the range is estimated to be between 170,000 and 250,000 while the estimates of breeding pairs are from 60,000 to 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_3-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>  It has the largest known range of any member of its family, with a range estimated at about 140 million square kilometers.  If its taxonomic <a href="/wiki/Accipitriformes" title="Accipitriformes">order</a> is considered, it is the second most wide-ranging species after only the <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">osprey</a> (<i>Pandion haliaetus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>  Few other eagle species are as numerous, though some species like tawny eagle, wedge-tailed eagle and bald eagle have total estimated populations of a similar size to the golden eagle's despite their more restricted distributions.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_6-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  The world's most populous eagle may be the <a href="/wiki/African_fish_eagle" title="African fish eagle">African fish eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus vocifer</i>), which has a stable total population estimated at 300,000 and is found solely in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On a global scale, the golden eagle is not considered threatened by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
